"""
Code modified from here
https://github.com/albanie/collaborative-experts/blob/master/model/net_vlad.py
"""


import math
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F
import torch as th


class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, cluster_size, feature_size, ghost_clusters):
        super(Model, self).__init__()

        self.feature_size = feature_size
        self.cluster_size = cluster_size
        self.ghost_clusters = ghost_clusters

        init_sc = (1 / math.sqrt(feature_size))
        clusters = cluster_size + ghost_clusters

        # The `clusters` weights are the `(w,b)` in the paper
        self.clusters = nn.Parameter(init_sc * th.randn(feature_size, clusters))
        self.batch_norm = nn.BatchNorm1d(clusters)
        # The `clusters2` weights are the visual words `c_k` in the paper
        self.clusters2 = nn.Parameter(init_sc * th.randn(1, feature_size, cluster_size))
        self.out_dim = self.cluster_size * feature_size

    def forward(self, x, mask=None):
        """Aggregates feature maps into a fixed size representation.  In the following
        notation, B = batch_size, N = num_features, K = num_clusters, D = feature_size.

        Args:
            x (th.Tensor): B x N x D

        Returns:
            (th.Tensor): B x DK
        """
        max_sample = x.size()[1]
        x = x.view(-1, self.feature_size)  # B x N x D -> BN x D

        if x.device != self.clusters.device:
            msg = f"x.device {x.device} != cluster.device {self.clusters.device}"
            raise ValueError(msg)

        assignment = th.matmul(x, self.clusters)  # (BN x D) x (D x (K+G)) -> BN x (K+G)
        assignment = self.batch_norm(assignment)

        assignment = F.softmax(assignment, dim=1)  # BN x (K+G) -> BN x (K+G)
        # remove ghost assigments
        assignment = assignment[:, :self.cluster_size]
        assignment = assignment.view(-1, max_sample, self.cluster_size)  # -> B x N x K
        a_sum = th.sum(assignment, dim=1, keepdim=True)  # B x N x K -> B x 1 x K
        a = a_sum * self.clusters2

        assignment = assignment.transpose(1, 2)  # B x N x K -> B x K x N

        x = x.view(-1, max_sample, self.feature_size)  # BN x D -> B x N x D
        vlad = th.matmul(assignment, x)  # (B x K x N) x (B x N x D) -> B x K x D
        vlad = vlad.transpose(1, 2)  # -> B x D x K
        vlad = vlad - a

        # L2 intra norm
        vlad = F.normalize(vlad)

        # flattening + L2 norm
        vlad = vlad.reshape(-1, self.cluster_size * self.feature_size)  # -> B x DK
        vlad = F.normalize(vlad)
        return vlad  # B x DK

batch_size = 2048
num_features = 100
num_clusters = 32
feature_size = 512
ghost_clusters = 16

def get_inputs():
  return [torch.rand(batch_size, num_features, feature_size)]

def get_init_inputs():
  return [num_clusters, feature_size, ghost_clusters]

reasoning
We need to write a custom CUDA kernel for a PyTorch model (NetVLAD-like). Let's understand the reference implementation first. Reference: - Input: x of shape (B, N, D) - max_sample = N - x is reshaped to (B*N, D) - assignment = x @ clusters # (BN, D) @ (D, K+G) -> (BN, K+G) - batch_norm on assignment (BatchNorm1d with K+G features) - softmax on assignment across dim=1 - assignment = assignment[:, :K] (remove ghost clusters) - reshape to (B, N, K) - a_sum = sum(assignment, dim=1, keepdim=True) # (B, 1, K) - a = a_sum * clusters2 # clusters2 shape (1, D, K), a_sum (B,1,K) -> (B, D, K) via broadcasting? Let's check. Actually in code: self.clusters2 = nn.Parameter(init_sc * th.randn(1, feature_size, cluster_size)) a_sum shape: (B, 1, K). a = a_sum * self.clusters2. In PyTorch, a_sum (B,1,K) broadcasts with clusters2 (1,D,K) => (B,D,K). So a has shape (B, D, K). - assignment = assignment.transpose(1,2) # (B, K, N) - x is reshaped to (B, N, D) - vlad = assignment @ x # (B, K, N) @ (B, N, D) -> (B, K, D) - vlad = vlad.transpose(1,2) # (B, D, K) - vlad = vlad - a # (B, D, K) - F.normalize(vlad) # L2 normalize over last dimension? Actually F.normalize default dim=1? Wait PyTorch F.normalize default dim=1? Let's check: torch.nn.functional.normalize(input, p=2.0, dim=1, eps=1e-12, out=None). Default dim=1. So for vlad shape (B, D, K), it normalizes along dim=1 (feature dimension D). So each (K) vector of length D is normalized. - flatten to (B, D*K) - F.normalize(vlad) again, default dim=1, so normalizes the flattened vector of length D*K. We need to write a custom CUDA kernel for this. The shapes are: - B = batch_size = 2048 - N = num_features = 100 - K = num_clusters = 32 - D = feature_size = 512 - G = ghost_clusters = 16 So assignment before ghost removal is (2048*100, 32+16) = (204800, 48). That's a big matrix multiply. But maybe we can fuse some operations into a single kernel or a few kernels to reduce memory bandwidth. However, the main heavy ops are: 1. matmul(x, clusters) -> (BN, D) @ (D, K+G) 2. batch_norm (can be fused) 3. softmax 4. sum over N 5. matmul(assignment, x) -> (B, K, N) @ (B, N, D) 6. normalize We can implement entire forward in one or a few kernels. Given the constraints, we need to produce numerically equivalent outputs (fp32). We can fuse batch norm and softmax and ghost removal into one kernel, then compute a_sum and vlad maybe in another kernel, then normalize. But since we have limited number of steps, we should think about performance. The GPU specs: let's get them.

Let me analyze the reference model to understand what I need to optimize. This is a NetVLAD implementation. The forward pass does: 1. `x = x.view(-1, self.feature_size)` - BN x D 2. `assignment = th.matmul(x, self.clusters)` - BN x (K+G) 3. `assignment = self.batch_norm(assignment)` - BN x (K+G) 4. `assignment = F.softmax(assignment, dim=1)` - BN x (K+G) 5. `assignment = assignment[:, :self.cluster_size]` - BN x K 6. `assignment = assignment.view(-1, max_sample, self.cluster_size)` - B x N x K 7. `a_sum = th.sum(assignment, dim=1, keepdim=True)` - B x 1 x K 8. `a = a_sum * self.clusters2` - B x 1 x K broadcast with 1 x D x K -> B x D x K 9. `assignment = assignment.transpose(1, 2)` - B x K x N 10. `x = x.view(-1, max_sample, self.feature_size)` - B x N x D 11. `vlad = th.matmul(assignment, x)` - B x K x D 12. `vlad = vlad.transpose(1, 2)` - B x D x K 13. `vlad = vlad - a` - B x D x K 14. `vlad = F.normalize(vlad)` - L2 normalize over last dim 15. `vlad = vlad.reshape(-1, self.cluster_size * self.feature_size)` - B x DK 16. `vlad = F.normalize(vlad)` - L2 normalize over last dim Given: - batch_size = 2048 - num_features = 100 - num_clusters = 32 - feature_size = 512 - ghost_clusters = 16 So B=2048, N=100, K=32, D=512, G=16 BN = 204800 Key operations: 1. Matmul: (204800, 512) @ (512, 48) -> quite large 2. BatchNorm1d across 48 features on 204800 samples 3. Softmax across 48 features on 204800 samples 4. Sum: (2048, 100, 32) -> (2048, 1, 32) 5. Broadcast multiply: (2048, 1, 32) * (1, 512, 32) -> (2048, 512, 32) 6. Matmul: (2048, 32, 100) @ (2048, 100, 512) -> (2048, 32, 512) 7. Two L2 normalizations This is heavily compute-bound for the matmuls, but also has memory bandwidth for the reductions and normalizations. To optimize this, I should fuse as many operations as possible into a single CUDA kernel, or at least minimize intermediate memory round-trips. Let me think about what can be fused: - The assignment computation: matmul(x, clusters) -> batch_norm -> softmax -> slice -> reshape -> transpose - The vlad computation: matmul(assignment, x) -> transpose -> subtract -> normalize -> reshape -> normalize Actually, the matmuls are the dominant operations. But there might be room for optimization by fusing the elementwise operations. The largest matrices: - x is 2048 x 100 x 512 = 104.9M elements - clusters is 512 x 48 = 24.6K elements - assignment after matmul is 204800 x 48 = 9.8M elements - vlad is 2048 x 32 x 512 = 33.6M elements The first matmul: (204800, 512) @ (512, 48) -> standard PyTorch uses cuBLAS which is well optimized. The second matmul: (2048, 32, 100) @ (2048, 100, 512) -> batched matmul, also well optimized by cuBLAS. So the main opportunity is fusing the elementwise operations between and around the matmuls to reduce memory bandwidth. But wait - can I rewrite this more efficiently? Let me think about the batch_norm and softmax on the assignment. The assignment after matmul is 204800 x 48. Batch norm and softmax are elementwise/reduction operations. Actually, the softmax across 48 elements per row with 204800 rows is relatively small. The batch norm is also relatively small. These could potentially be fused with the matmul if I computed the matmul manually, but cuBLAS matmuls are very fast. Let me think more carefully. 204800 x 512 x 48 = about 5B multiply-adds for the first matmul. At 67 TFLOPS, that's ~75ms (but TF32 Tensor Cores could do 989 TFLOPS). Actually for fp32, standard FP32 is 67 TFLOPS on H100, but Tensor Cores with TF32 can do much more. PyTorch likely uses TF32 when allowed, or fp32 cuBLAS. Actually, wait. On H100, for matmuls, PyTorch typically uses cuBLAS which can leverage Tensor Cores for fp32 via TF32 if enabled, or use the standard FP32 path. The second matmul: B x K x N @ B x N x D for B=2048, K=32, N=100, D=512 This is a batched matrix multiply. Ops per batch: 32 x 100 x 512 = 1.64M Total: 2048 x 1.64M = 3.36B ops At 67 TFLOPS = 0.05ms At 494 TFLOPS = 0.007ms So both matmuls combined are maybe ~0.1-0.2ms. The overhead comes from all the intermediate memory operations. The real optimization opportunity is fusing all the elementwise operations to avoid HBM round-trips. Hmm, let me think about what operations dominate the memory bandwidth. In eval mode, batchnorm is elementwise. The sequence is: - matmul: x @ clusters -> assignment_large (computes in registers, writes to HBM) - batchnorm: reads assignment_large, writes assignment_bn - softmax: reads assignment_bn, writes assignment_sm - slice: reads assignment_sm, writes assignment_slice (no actual copy if using slicing, but view) - reshape: view only - transpose: might copy if not contiguous So the memory traffic without fusing: 1. assignment (BN x 48): write ~40MB (204800 * 48 * 4 bytes) 2. batchnorm: read 40MB, write 40MB 3. softmax: read 40MB, write 40MB 4. slice, reshape: view 5. transpose: might make contiguous or might not. For a kernel fusion strategy, I think the best approach is: 1. Keep first matmul in PyTorch (cuBLAS is optimal for this size) 2. Fuse BN + softmax + slice + reshape + transpose into one kernel. - Actually, for the second matmul we need assignment as (B, K, N). We could compute that layout directly. 3. Keep second bmm in PyTorch 4. Fuse transpose + subtract + normalize + reshape + normalize into one kernel. But actually, we can do even better. Let me think about whether we can write custom kernels that are competitive with cuBLAS. For the first matmul: (204800, 512) @ (512, 48) This is a "tall and skinny" matrix multiply. The output is 204800 x 48. PyTorch/cuBLAS handles this well. But for a custom kernel, we could: - Load clusters into shared memory (512 x 48 x 4 = 98KB) - Each thread block processes a chunk of rows from x - Compute the matrix multiply, apply batch norm, softmax, and slice all in registers - Write out the reduced assignment (B x K x N) Wait, the assignment after softmax needs to be used in the second matmul anyway. So writing it to HBM is necessary unless we fuse the second matmul too. Could we fuse the entire computation into one kernel? Let's think about the compute pattern: 1. For each element in the batch B and feature N: - Compute dot product of x[b,n,:] with each of the 48 cluster centers - Apply batch norm (48 elementwise ops) - Apply softmax (48 exp + reductions) - Discard ghost clusters, keep 32 values 2. Then for each batch: - Sum assignment over N -> a_sum (K) - Compute a = a_sum * clusters2 (D x K elementwise) - Compute vlad = assignment^T @ x (K x N @ N x D) -> K x D - Transpose to D x K - Subtract a - Normalize (D x K) - Reshape and normalize (D*K) If we fuse everything, the only HBM reads are x (input), clusters, clusters2, batchnorm params. The only HBM write is the final vlad output. This would be compute-heavy but memory-bandwidth optimal. Can we write a fused kernel? Let's think about the shared memory and registers. For each batch element b: - We need to process x[b, :, :] which is N x D = 100 x 512 - We need to compute assignment_softmax[b, :, :] = softmax(BN(x[b,:,:] @ clusters))[:, :K] where x[b,:,:] is (100, 512), clusters is (512, 48) So local matmul: (100, 512) @ (512, 48) = (100, 48) Each batch element requires 100 x 48 = 4800 assignment values. Then we need a_sum over N: 32 reductions of 100 elements each. Then vlad = assignment^T @ x: (32, 100) @ (100, 512) = (32, 512) So per batch element: - Matmul 1: 100 x 512 x 48 = 2.46M multiply-adds - Softmax: 100 x 48 exp and reductions - Sum: 32 x 100 additions - Matmul 2: 32 x 100 x 512 = 1.64M multiply-adds - Elementwise ops: D x K = 16384 ops Total per batch: ~4.1M FMAs + overheads For B=2048: ~8.4B FMAs total If we process one batch element per block or a few per block... Actually, processing batch elements independently is great because B=2048 gives us a lot of parallelism. Let's design a kernel where each thread block handles one batch element b: **Block design for one batch element:** - We have x[b, :, :] = (100, 512) - We want to compute vlad output of size (D*K,) = 16384 Step 1: Load clusters (512, 48) into shared memory. 512 * 48 * 4 bytes = 98,304 bytes. This fits in shared memory (227KB max per block). Step 2: For each of the N=100 features, compute the 48 dot products. We can parallelize over the 48 cluster dimensions. With 1024 threads: - Each thread computes ~5 dot products (5 out of 48, for a specific n). - Actually, better: launch 48 threads (or 64) per n, with n looped over 100. - With 1024 threads, we can do n in parallel too. Actually, let's think more carefully about matmul 1. x is (100, 512), clusters is (512, 48) We want assignment (100, 48) With 1024 threads per block: Option A: Each thread computes one element of the output. That's 100 * 48 = 4800 elements. We'd need ~5 blocks per batch, or we could use fewer threads. Actually 4800 elements with 1024 threads means each thread does ~5 output elements, but we only have one block. Option B: Use more blocks. A block per batch element is nice. Within the block, compute the (100, 48) output using 1024 threads. The reduction dimension is 512. For one output element (n, c): dot(x[n, :], clusters[:, c]) We can parallelize the dot product reduction using threads. Let's say we have 1024 threads. For each n in 0..99: For c in 0..47: assignment[n, c] = dot(x[n, :], clusters[:, c]) We have 4800 outputs. Each requires 512 multiply-adds. Total work: 4800 * 512 = 2,457,600 FMAs. With 1024 threads, each thread does ~2400 FMAs. That's good. But we also need shared memory for x[b, :, :] which is 100*512*4 = 204,800 bytes. And clusters in shared memory: 512*48*4 = 98,304 bytes. Total shared memory: ~303KB. That's over the 227KB limit per block! So we can't fit both x and clusters in shared memory. Alternative: Keep clusters in shared memory (98KB). Stream x through shared memory or registers. We can load rows of x into shared memory or registers as needed. Better approach: - Load clusters into shared memory: 98KB. - For each row n of x (512 floats = 2KB): - Load x[b, n, :] into shared memory or registers (all threads participate) - Compute assignment[b, n, c] for all c = 0..47 using the cluster centers in SMEM - Store assignment in registers or SMEM Actually, we need assignment for matmul 2 later. But we can keep it in registers if we process carefully. Wait, matmul 2 is assignment^T @ x. That means we need to access assignment[:, c] (the c-th cluster across all n) and x[n, d] (the d-th dimension across all n). If we compute assignment row by row and immediately use it for matmul 2, we can't do it easily because matmul 2 needs all assignment values. Actually, we can compute assignment row by row and accumulate into vlad: vlad[c, d] = sum_n assignment[n, c] * x[n, d] So if we process n=0..99 one at a time: 1. Load x[b, n, :] (512 floats) 2. Compute assignment[n, c] for c=0..47 3. Apply BN, softmax to get assignment[n, c] 4. For each c and d: vlad[c, d] += assignment[n, c] * x[n, d] This is perfect! We don't need to store the full assignment matrix. We just need to accumulate vlad[c, d] in registers or SMEM. vlad is (32, 512) = 16,384 floats = 64KB. This can be kept in registers distributed across threads, or in shared memory. With 1024 threads and 16,384 elements: - Each thread could hold 16 floats for vlad accumulation. - 16,384 / 1024 = 16 elements per thread. This seems very doable! Let's outline the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void netvlad_kernel( const float* x, // B x N x D const float* clusters, // D x (K+G) const float* bn_weight, const float* bn_bias, const float* bn_rm, const float* bn_rv, float bn_eps, const float* clusters2, // 1 x D x K float* output, // B x (D*K) int B, int N, int D, int K, int G ) { int b = blockIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; extern __shared__ float smem[]; // clusters in smem: D x (K+G) // x_row in smem: D // assignment_row in smem or regs: (K+G) int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; // Load clusters into shared memory float* clusters_smem = smem; int total_clusters = D * (K + G); for (int i = tid; i < total_clusters; i += num_threads) { clusters_smem[i] = clusters[i]; } __syncthreads(); // vlad accumulator: K x D floats // We can accumulate in local registers. Each thread handles a subset. // But K*D = 32*512 = 16384. With 1024 threads, each thread handles 16 elements. // Actually, let's use shared memory for vlad if possible. // 16384 * 4 = 64KB. Plus clusters (98KB) = 162KB. This fits in shared memory! // Wait, per-block shared memory is up to 227KB. So: // clusters: D*(K+G)*4 = 512*48*4 = 98,304 bytes // vlad: K*D*4 = 32*512*4 = 65,536 bytes // x_row: D*4 = 512*4 = 2,048 bytes // Total: ~166KB. This fits! float* vlad_smem = clusters_smem + D * (K + G); float* x_row_smem = vlad_smem + K * D; // Initialize vlad to 0 for (int i = tid; i < K * D; i += num_threads) { vlad_smem[i] = 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); // assignment and a_sum // a_sum is K floats. We can compute it as we go. // Actually a_sum[c] = sum_n assignment[n, c] // Since we process n sequentially, we can accumulate a_sum in local variables. // K=32, so a_sum is small. float a_sum[32]; // per-thread? No, we need to share a_sum. // Actually a_sum is just 32 floats. Put in SMEM. float* a_sum_smem = x_row_smem + D; if (tid < K) a_sum_smem[tid] = 0.0f; __syncthreads(); // For each n for (int n = 0; n < N; n++) { // Load x[b, n, d] into x_row_smem const float* x_ptr = x + b * N * D + n * D; for (int d = tid; d < D; d += num_threads) { x_row_smem[d] = x_ptr[d]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute assignment for this n: D dot products with clusters // assignment[c] = sum_d x_row[d] * clusters[d, c] // We can parallelize over c. Each thread computes some c's. float assignment[48]; // too much for registers? 48 floats per thread. // With 1024 threads, if each thread handles 1 c, we have enough threads. // But c goes from 0 to 47. // Actually, with 1024 threads, we can have thread c compute assignment[c] for all n. // But we process n sequentially. Let's have thread c compute: // assignment_c = sum_d x_row[d] * clusters[d, c] // for c = tid, tid+num_threads, ... but only 48 values. float local_assignment[48]; // per thread: only if tid < 48 // Better: each thread computes one or more c values. // Let's say threads 0..511 each compute one c? No, 48 < 1024. // We can have thread c (for c < 48) compute assignment[c], or we can parallelize the dot product. // For c < 48: compute dot product over D=512. if (tid < K + G) { float val = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { val += x_row_smem[d] * clusters_smem[d * (K + G) + tid]; } // Apply batch norm // val = (val - rm) / sqrt(rv + eps) * weight + bias val = (val - bn_rm[tid]) / sqrtf(bn_rv[tid] + bn_eps) * bn_weight[tid] + bn_bias[tid]; local_assignment = val; // but we need all 48 for softmax // Problem: each thread only has one value. Need all-to-all for softmax. } // Softmax requires all 48 values. With 1024 threads, we can use shared memory. float* assign_smem = x_row_smem; // reuse x_row_smem after computing? No, need x for dot products. // Better: use a separate part of smem for assignment. } } ``` Hmm, the problem is the softmax requires all 48 assignment values to compute the max and sum. Actually, since K+G=48 is so small, we could even have each thread loop over all c values: For n = tid to N-1 stepping by blockDim.x: load x[b, n, :] for c = 0 to 47: compute dot(x[b,n,:], clusters[:,c]) apply BN and softmax store assignment[n, c] for c=0..31 accumulate a_sum and vlad But this requires each thread to hold the full x[b, n, :] and the full assignment row. x[row] is 512 floats = 512 registers if kept in registers, too many. We can keep x[b, n, :] in shared memory (one copy per block). And assignment row (48 floats) in registers per thread? Or in shared memory. Wait, if tid processes different n values simultaneously, we need multiple x rows in SMEM or we need each thread to load from HBM directly. With 1024 threads, if each processes a different n, we'd need 1024 x rows in SMEM = 1024 * 512 * 4 = 2MB. Too much. So we should process n in groups. Like: for n_chunk = 0 to N-1 stepping by some group size. But N is only 100. And if we use block size 256, then we could process up to 256 n values. But we only have 100. Actually, maybe use a smaller block size like 256 threads? With 256 threads per block, we can fit more blocks per SM. Block per batch element: 2048 blocks. Each block: 256 threads = 8 warps. With 256 threads: - Load x row into SMEM: 512 floats, 256 threads load 2 each. - For c computation: have 256 threads compute up to 48 c values. We could have groups of 4-8 threads per c. But smaller block size means fewer threads to parallelize over c. However, 256 threads is still plenty for 48 dot products. Actually, let me think about the total shared memory again. With block size 256, 48 SMs (just estimating), each SM can run multiple blocks. Shared memory per block is ~166KB. Each SM has 227KB shared memory. So each SM can run at most 1 block at a time. With 132 SMs, total concurrent blocks = 132. Total blocks = 2048. Waves = 2048 / 132 = 15.5 waves. Each wave processes one batch element per SM. Time per batch element = total work / SM throughput. At 67 TFLOPS FP32 across 132 SMs, each SMs does ~0.5 TFLOPS. Work per batch element: 2.4M FMAs (matmul 1) + 1.64M FMAs (matmul 2) = ~4M FMAs. At 0.5 TFLOPS per SM: 8 microseconds per batch element. With 15.5 waves: 124 microseconds. Plus memory overhead. With block size 1024: Still only 1 block per SM (due to shared memory). Warps per block: 32. SMs can handle up to 64 warps (I think on H100, up to 2048 threads per SM). So 1 block of 1024 = 32 warps fits easily. Total concurrent blocks = 132. Waves = 2048 / 132 = 15.5. Work per batch element is the same, but now 1024 threads per batch means potentially more parallel. Actually with 1024 threads and only 48 active threads for the c-dot-products, we waste a lot. But if we split the dot product reduction, we can use more threads. Let me try to build the kernel step by step. ```cuda __global__ void netvlad_forward( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ clusters, const float* __restrict__ bn_weight, const float* __restrict__ bn_bias, const float* __restrict__ bn_rm, const float* __restrict__ bn_rv, float bn_eps, const float* __restrict__ clusters2, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int N, int D, int K, int G ) { extern __shared__ float smem[]; int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int block_size = blockDim.x; // SMEM layout: // clusters: D * (K+G) // vlad: K * D // x_row: D // a_sum: K float* clusters_smem = smem; float* vlad_smem = clusters_smem + D * (K + G); float* x_row_smem = vlad_smem + K * D; float* a_sum_smem = x_row_smem + D; // Load clusters into SMEM int total_cg = D * (K + G); for (int i = tid; i < total_cg; i += block_size) { clusters_smem[i] = clusters[i]; } // Load clusters2 into SMEM (optional, could read from global) // Actually clusters2 is small: D*K = 16384 floats = 64KB. // Might be fine to keep in global memory or SMEM. // Let's load it at the end or not at all. // Initialize vlad and a_sum for (int i = tid; i < K * D; i += block_size) { vlad_smem[i] = 0.0f; } for (int i = tid; i < K; i += block_size) { a_sum_smem[i] = 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); // Process each n for (int n = 0; n < N; n++) { // Load x[b, n, d] into x_row_smem const float* x_ptr = x + b * N * D + n * D; for (int d = tid; d < D; d += block_size) { x_row_smem[d] = x_ptr[d]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute assignment for this n // We need to compute D dot products for c=0..(K+G-1) // Strategy: each thread computes 1 or more c's by looping over d. // Since K+G=48, let's have a subset of threads compute dot products. // Actually, to use all threads, we can parallelize over both c and d. // But the result is assignment[n, c] for c=0..47. // With block_size=1024 and K+G=48, we can use 48 warps (1536 threads) or // just have 48 groups of threads. // Simpler: since total FMAs is small (48*512 = 24K), we can have // 48 threads each do 512 FMAs. // But then softmax needs those 48 values. // Let's use a warp (32 threads) to compute 2 c values, or similar. // Actually, let's just have tid < (K+G) compute assignment[c]. // 48 out of 1024 threads will be active. For 100 iterations, each doing // 24K FMAs with 48 threads = 500 FMAs per active thread = negligible. // The GPU will be mostly idle during computation and waiting on memory. // Better to use more threads: each thread computes a partial sum. // We can assign groups of threads to each c. // Let group_size = block_size / (K+G) = 1024/48 = 21.3. Not even. // Let group_size = 16. Then groups = 1024/16 = 64. // We only need 48 c's, so 48 groups of 16 threads = 768 threads. // Each thread in group c computes partial dot product over D/16 = 32 elements. // Then warp-level reduction (within the 16 threads). int group_size = 16; int num_groups = block_size / group_size; // 64 int group_id = tid / group_size; int lane_id = tid % group_size; if (group_id < (K + G)) { int c = group_id; float val = 0.0f; // Each thread in the group handles D/group_size elements int d_per_thread = (D + group_size - 1) / group_size; // 32 for (int i = 0; i < d_per_thread; i++) { int d = lane_id + i * group_size; if (d < D) { // clusters_smem is column-major or row-major? // clusters in PyTorch is (feature_size, clusters) = (D, K+G) // So clusters[d, c] is at d*(K+G) + c val += x_row_smem[d] * clusters_smem[d * (K+G) + c]; } } // Reduce within the group (16 threads) // Using warp shuffle #pragma unroll for (int offset = group_size/2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } if (lane_id == 0) { // Apply batch norm val = (val - bn_rm[c]) / sqrtf(bn_rv[c] + bn_eps) * bn_weight[c] + bn_bias[c]; // Store in a temporary SMEM location for softmax x_row_smem[D + c] = val; // Reuse x_row_smem + D as assignment_smem } } __syncthreads(); // Now all 48 assignment values are in x_row_smem[D..D+47] // Softmax if (tid < (K + G)) { // Find max float max_val = -1e20f; for (int c = 0; c < (K+G); c++) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, x_row_smem[D + c]); } // Compute exp and sum float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < (K+G); c++) { float e = expf(x_row_smem[D + c] - max_val); x_row_smem[D + c] = e; sum_exp += e; } // Normalize for (int c = 0; c < (K+G); c++) { x_row_smem[D + c] /= sum_exp; } } __syncthreads(); // Now x_row_smem[D+c] holds softmax assignment[n, c] for c=0..47 // Accumulate a_sum and vlad // For c=0..(K-1), a_sum[c] += assignment[n, c] // For vlad: vlad[c, d] += assignment[n, c] * x[n, d] // Strategy: parallelize over (c, d). // K*D = 16384 elements. Each thread handles some of them. // But assignment[n, c] varies with c. // For a fixed d, threads can process all c. // Better: have threads loop over c and d. if (tid < K) { float a_val = x_row_smem[D + tid]; atomicAdd(&a_sum_smem[tid], a_val); } // vlad accumulation: vlad[c, d] += assignment[n, c] * x_row_smem[d] // With 1024 threads, each thread can handle ~16 (c,d) pairs. // Total pairs: 32 * 512 = 16384. // We'll loop with stride block_size. for (int idx = tid; idx < K * D; idx += block_size) { int c = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float a_val = x_row_smem[D + c]; atomicAdd(&vlad_smem[c * D + d], a_val * x_row_smem[d]); } __syncthreads(); } // After processing all n, vlad_smem[c*D + d] = sum_n assignment[n,c] * x[n,d] // a_sum_smem[c] = sum_n assignment[n,c] // Compute vlad = vlad_smem - a_sum_smem * clusters2 // Then normalize. // Load clusters2? clusters2 is (1, D, K). We'll read from global. // First: vlad[c, d] -= a_sum[c] * clusters2[d, c] for (int idx = tid; idx < K * D; idx += block_size) { int c = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float v = vlad_smem[idx]; v -= a_sum_smem[c] * clusters2[d * K + c]; // clusters2 layout: (D, K) vlad_smem[idx] = v; } __syncthreads(); // Normalize 1: per row d, normalize over c (K elements) // vlad is stored as c*D + d, so for fixed d, elements are at d, D+d, 2*D+d, ... // We need to compute sum_c vlad[c, d]^2 for each d. // With block_size threads, each thread handles some d values. // D = 512. for (int d = tid; d < D; d += block_size) { float sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < K; c++) { float v = vlad_smem[c * D + d]; sq_sum += v * v; } float norm = sqrtf(sq_sum); float inv_norm = norm > 1e-12f ? 1.0f / norm : 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < K; c++) { vlad_smem[c * D + d] *= inv_norm; } } __syncthreads(); // Normalize 2: per batch element, normalize over all D*K elements float sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < K * D; idx += block_size) { float v = vlad_smem[idx]; sq_sum += v * v; } // Reduce sq_sum across block // Warp reduce #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sq_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq_sum, offset); } // Broadcast from lane 0 to all threads in warp sq_sum = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq_sum, 0); float norm = sqrtf(sq_sum); float inv_norm = norm > 1e-12f ? 1.0f / norm : 0.0f; // Write output float* out_ptr = output + b * K * D; for (int idx = tid; idx < K * D; idx += block_size) { out_ptr[idx] = vlad_smem[idx] * inv_norm; } } ``` Wait, there are several issues with this kernel: 1. The `atomicAdd` in the inner loop will be very slow. We have 16384 atomicAdds per n, for N=100. That's 1.6M atomicAdds per block! That's terrible. 2. The atomicAdd on `a_sum_smem` is also slow, but K=32 so only 32 atomics per n. 3. The vlad accumulation should avoid atomics. Instead, we can have each thread maintain a local accumulator for the (c, d) pairs it handles, and since each thread processes a unique set of (c, d) pairs and a single n at a time, we don't need atomics if each thread accumulates its own slice. Wait, but vlad needs to accumulate across all n. If a thread handles the same (c, d) pair across all n, it can keep a register accumulator. Revised approach: assign each thread to a fixed set of (c, d) pairs. For example, thread i handles indices idx = i, i+block_size, i+2*block_size, ... in the flattened K*D array. Each such idx corresponds to a fixed (c, d). For each n, the thread reads assignment[n, c] from SMEM and x[n, d] from SMEM, multiplies, and adds to its register accumulator. This is perfect! No atomics needed. But each thread needs to read assignment[n, c] for its specific c. Since assignment[n, :] is in SMEM (48 floats), all reads are fast. So corrected: ```cuda // Register accumulator for each thread float vlad_local[16]; // if each thread handles up to 16 elements // Actually we can just use a single float per (c,d) pair, but each thread might handle ~16 pairs. // 16384 / 1024 = 16 pairs per thread. We need 16 registers. // Better: just accumulate in an array of floats indexed by a local counter. // Or simpler: loop over indices with stride, and accumulate into a running sum. ``` Actually, we don't need an array. The thread loops over n. Wait, if we restructure to have the n loop outside the idx loop, then each thread processes all n for its assigned (c,d) pairs. But computing assignment[n, c] for all c requires all 48 dot products. If each thread only needs one c, we'd compute redundant dot products. Maybe the most efficient is: - A subgroup of threads computes assignment[n, :] and stores in SMEM. - Then ALL threads participate in vlad accumulation using their local (c,d) registers. But the assignment needs to be computed first. Let me think... Actually, since K+G=48 is small, maybe we can have each thread compute the entire assignment row for each n. Wait, vlad accumulation is: for each (c,d), acc += a[c] * x[d]. That's 1 FMA per idx per n. Total FMAs for vlad accumulation: N * K * D = 100 * 32 * 512 = 1.64M. With 1024 threads: 1600 FMAs per thread. Plus dot products: N * 48 * 512 = 2.46M FMAs. So total FMAs: 4.1M. If we have 768 threads for dot products (48 groups of 16): 2.46M / 768 = 3200 FMAs per active thread. And 1024 threads for vlad accumulation: 1.64M / 1024 = 1600 FMAs per thread. But since these happen sequentially within the n loop, the total per-thread work is 3200 + 1600 = 4800 FMAs. At 67 TFLOPS for the whole GPU with 132 SMs, that's actually fine. The theoretical min time is 4.1M / 67T = 61 microseconds for compute. But the atomicAdd issue is critical. Let me redesign to avoid atomics. Revised n-loop structure: ```cuda // Each thread is assigned to a subset of (c, d) pairs // For each n, we compute assignment[n, c] and store in shared memory // Then each thread updates its local accumulator // But assignment[n, c] depends on n. We need fresh assignment for each n. // Approach: // 1. First, for all n, compute assignment[n, c] and store somewhere. // This would need B*N*K floats = 2048*100*48 = 9.8M floats = 39MB of storage. // If we process batch-by-batch, per batch we need 100*48 = 4800 floats = 19KB. // We could store assignment in shared memory? 4800 * 4 = 19KB. // That's small enough! Let me write the revised kernel: 1. In the n loop: a. Load x[b, n, :] into x_row_smem b. Compute assignment[n, c] for c=0..K+G-1 using a subset of threads, store in assignment_smem c. __syncthreads() d. If tid < K, accumulate a_sum[c] (using atomicAdd or... actually a_sum is just K floats. We can have each thread that owns c=0..K-1 accumulate a_sum similar to vlad. The kernel is going to be memory-bandwidth-bound, not compute-bound. Let me write a simple version that works first, then optimize. Here's a pragmatic design: ```cuda __global__ void netvlad_forward(...) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int block_size = blockDim.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* clusters_smem = smem; float* vlad_smem = clusters_smem + D * (K + G); float* x_row_smem = vlad_smem + K * D; float* assignment_smem = x_row_smem + D; float* a_sum_smem = assignment_smem + (K + G); // Load clusters (D x (K+G)) for (int i = tid; i < D * (K + G); i += block_size) { clusters_smem[i] = clusters[i]; } if (tid < K) a_sum_smem[tid] = 0.0f; // Zero vlad for (int i = tid; i < K * D; i += block_size) { vlad_smem[i] = 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); // Each thread will accumulate a few vlad elements // We assign idx = tid, tid + block_size, ... in flattened K*D int num_idx_per_thread = (K * D + block_size - 1) / block_size; // Actually we'll just handle the strided loop // Register accumulators for vlad float acc[16]; // max 16 per thread int acc_count = 0; for (int idx = tid; idx < K * D; idx += block_size) { acc[acc_count++] = 0.0f; } for (int n = 0; n < N; n++) { // Load x const float* x_ptr = x + b * N * D + n * D; for (int d = tid; d < D; d += block_size) { x_row_smem[d] = __ldg(x_ptr + d); } __syncthreads(); // Compute assignment[0..K+G-1] // Use all threads: each thread handles one c, parallelize d reduction // Actually simpler: each thread computes dot product for c = tid if tid < K+G if (tid < (K + G)) { float val = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { val += x_row_smem[d] * clusters_smem[d * (K+G) + tid]; } // BN val = (val - bn_rm[tid]) * rsqrtf(bn_rv[tid] + bn_eps) * bn_weight[tid] + bn_bias[tid]; assignment_smem[tid] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Softmax if (tid == 0) { float max_val = assignment_smem[0]; for (int c = 1; c < (K+G); c++) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, assignment_smem[c]); } float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < (K+G); c++) { float e = expf(assignment_smem[c] - max_val); assignment_smem[c] = e; sum_exp += e; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int c = 0; c < (K+G); c++) { assignment_smem[c] *= inv_sum; } } __syncthreads(); // Accumulate a_sum if (tid < K) { atomicAdd(&a_sum_smem[tid], assignment_smem[tid]); } // Accumulate vlad using local registers acc_count = 0; for (int idx = tid; idx < K * D; idx += block_size) { int c = idx / D; int d = idx % D; acc[acc_count++] += assignment_smem[c] * x_row_smem[d]; } __syncthreads(); } // Write local accumulators to vlad_smem acc_count = 0; for (int idx = tid; idx < K * D; idx += block_size) { vlad_smem[idx] = acc[acc_count++]; } __syncthreads(); // Subtract a_sum * clusters2 for (int idx = tid; idx < K * D; idx += block_size) { int c = idx / D; int d = idx % D; vlad_smem[idx] -= a_sum_smem[c] * clusters2[d * K + c]; // clusters2: (D, K) } __syncthreads(); // Normalize 1: per d, over c for (int d = tid; d < D; d += block_size) { float sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < K; c++) { float v = vlad_smem[c * D + d]; sq_sum += v * v; } float inv_norm = rsqrtf(sq_sum + 1e-12f); for (int c = 0; c < K; c++) { vlad_smem[c * D + d] *= inv_norm; } } __syncthreads(); // Normalize 2: over all D*K float sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < K * D; idx += block_size) { float v = vlad_smem[idx]; sq_sum += v * v; } // Warp reduction #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sq_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq_sum, offset); } // Broadcast to all threads in warp sq_sum = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sq_sum, 0); float inv_norm = rsqrtf(sq_sum + 1e-12f); float* out_ptr = output + b * K * D; for (int idx = tid; idx < K * D; idx += block_size) { out_ptr[idx] = vlad_smem[idx] * inv_norm; } } ``` Issues: 1. The `acc` array needs a fixed size. `num_idx_per_thread = ceil(16384/1024) = 16`. So `acc[16]` is enough. 2. In the softamx, only 1 thread computes it. That's fine since it's only 48 elements. 3. The atomicAdd for a_sum is only 32 per n. 3200 total per block. Should be OK but not ideal. 4. The assignment computation with only 48 active threads is wasteful. But it's small. Actually, for correctness, I need to be careful with the batchnorm. The reference `self.batch_norm = nn.BatchNorm1d(clusters)` in training mode computes mean and variance over the batch dimension. But in the custom kernel, I'm treating it as eval mode (using stored running stats). Wait, in the reference forward, if the model is in training mode, batch norm computes statistics over the current input. If in eval mode, it uses running statistics. Since the test might call `model.train()` or `model.eval()`, I need to know which one. Looking at the reference code, there's no explicit train/eval call. When instantiated, nn.Module defaults to train mode. So `self.batch_norm` would be in training mode, using batch statistics. However, in many kernel optimization benchmarks, the reference is compared in eval mode, or they might not care about the difference because they use `torch.no_grad()` but not necessarily `eval()`. Wait, I should check if there's a way to determine this. The problem says "Replace the forward computation... Produce numerically equivalent outputs." If the reference model is in training mode and the test runs it in training mode, then I need to use batch statistics too. For correctness, I can check if the reference uses training or eval mode. But I don't know the test harness. Here's a safe approach: Implement batch norm as it would be in PyTorch: - Compute mean over the batch for each feature - Compute variance over the batch - Normalize using batch stats - Scale and shift This is more expensive but necessary if the model is in training mode. Wait, but then there's also the running mean/variance update. The reference updates `running_mean` and `running_var`. To match numerically on a single forward pass, the running stats update doesn't affect the output, only future calls. So we can ignore the running stats update for output matching. Since 512*48 = 24576, and we have 1024 threads: Each thread computes ~24 products. Then we need to reduce 512 partial sums per c down to 1. To make reduction efficient, we can use shared memory: - First, each thread writes its partial sum to a buffer. - Then we do parallel reduction per c. But this is getting complicated. Let me try a simpler GEMV approach: ```cuda // GEMV: x_row (D) @ clusters (D, C) -> assignment (C) // Using 1024 threads // We process d in chunks. Let me try a better approach for the GEMV within the n loop: For a single n, we compute 48 dot products. Instead of 1 thread per c, let's use all 1024 threads in a tiled matrix multiplication. x_row is 1 x 512 clusters is 512 x 48 We can view this as: the result is 48 floats. Divide the 512 dimension into chunks. Let's say we use 16 threads per c. 48 c's * 16 threads = 768 threads. Each of the 16 threads computes 512/16 = 32 elements. Then warp shuffle reduce within the 16 threads. With 768 active threads out of 1024, that's 75% utilization. And each thread does 32 FMAs, which is very little. Then the warp shuffle reduces across 16 threads. The whole thing should be very fast. Implementation: ```cuda int group_size = 16; // threads per c int num_groups = block_size / group_size; // 64 int group_id = tid / group_size; int lane = tid % group_size; if (group_id < (K + G)) { int c = group_id; float val = 0.0f; for (int d = lane; d < D; d += group_size) { val += x_row_smem[d] * clusters_smem[d * (K+G) + c]; } // Reduce within group #pragma unroll for (int offset = group_size/2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } if (lane == 0) { // BN val = (val - bn_rm[c]) * rsqrtf(bn_rv[c] + bn_eps) * bn_weight[c] + bn_bias[c]; assignment_smem[c] = val; } } __syncthreads(); // Softmax by one thread if (tid == 0) { ... } __syncthreads(); ``` This is quite clean! And 768 threads active for a small amount of work. Then all 1024 threads do the vlad accumulation. One concern: `__shfl_down_sync` requires all threads in the mask to participate. We use `0xFFFFFFFF` which is a full warp. But our group_size is 16, which is half a warp. Threads in the same warp but different groups (e.g., lane 0-15 for c=0, lane 16-31 for c=1) - the shuffle will work because we use the full warp mask. But threads from lane 16-31 will also participate in the shuffle for lanes 0-15... Wait, actually `__shfl_down_sync` with mask 0xFFFFFFFF requires all threads in the warp to be active or at least not cause divergence issues. If `group_id < 48`, then for warp 0 (threads 0-31), group_id 0 and 1 are < 48, so lanes 0-31 are all active. That's one warp handling c=0 and c=1. Warp 1 handles c=2 and c=3, etc. Up to warp 23 handles c=46 and c=47. Warp 24-31: group_id >= 48, so these warps are all inactive. The `if (group_id < (K+G))` means warps 0-23 are active, warps 24-31 are inactive. Within active warps: no divergence! Because lanes 0-15 have group_id = warp_id*2, lanes 16-31 have group_id = warp_id*2 + 1. Both are < 48 for warp_id <= 23. For warp 23: group_id = 46 and 47, both < 48. Active. For warp 24: group_id = 48 and 49, both >= 48. Inactive. So the branch is at warp granularity. No divergence within warps. Great! But the warp shuffle: within warp 0, lanes 0-15 do shfl_down for c=0, and lanes 16-31 do shfl_down for c=1. Since we use mask 0xFFFFFFFF, ALL lanes in the warp must execute the shfl instruction. But lanes 0-15 and lanes 16-31 are executing different shfl operations? Actually, they're executing the same instruction but different data. The `__shfl_down_sync` primitive handles this fine as long as all threads in the mask participate. Wait, actually each lane is doing shfl_down with lane_delta. Lane 0 gets from lane 8, lane 1 gets from lane 9, etc. Lane 16 gets from lane 24, etc. This is fine because the source and destination are both within the active mask. But the data comes from the wrong group! Lane 0 (c=0) will read from lane 8 (c=0), good. Lane 16 (c=1) reads from lane 24 (c=1), good. Since both groups are in the same warp and executing together, and the shfl offset is uniform across the warp (we use `#pragma unroll` with constant offsets), the hardware will shuffle within the warp correctly. Each lane reads from lane + offset. Lane 0 reads from 8, lane 16 reads from 24. Both are fine. But wait - the values for lanes 0-15 and 16-31 are independent. When doing `val += __shfl_down_sync(...)`, does lane 0 potentially get the value from lane 16 if offset causes wrap-around? No, shfl_down is from `src = tid + offset` within the warp. For lane 0 and offset 8: src = 8. Good. For lane 16 and offset 8: src = 24. Good. So it's fine. However, since we use `#pragma unroll` with `group_size/2 = 8`, `offset = 4`, etc.: Initial val: lanes 0-15 have their partial sums for c=0, lanes 16-31 for c=1. offset=8: lane 0 gets from lane 8 (same c), lane 16 gets from lane 24 (same c). Good. offset=4: lane 0 gets from lane 4, lane 16 gets from lane 20. Good. offset=2: lane 0 gets from lane 2, lane 16 gets from lane 18. Good. offset=1: lane 0 gets from lane 1, lane 16 gets from lane 17. Good. At offset=1, lane 15 gets from lane 16! But lane 16 belongs to a different c! This is a problem! Wait, `__shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, 1)` means lane i gets from lane i+1. For lane 15: gets from lane 16. But lane 16 computed the dot product for c=1, not c=0. So lane 15 would add the wrong value! This is a real issue. The shfl_down operates on the entire warp, not just our subgroup. To fix this, we have a few options: 1. Use smaller groups that align with warp boundaries. E.g., group_size = 32. - 48 groups of 32 threads = 1536 threads > 1024. Doesn't fit. 2. Use `__shfl_down_sync` with a subgroup mask. But CUDA shuffle doesn't directly support arbitrary masks for down operations. Actually, we can use `__shfl_down_sync(mask, val, offset)` where `mask` represents the active threads. But in CUDA, threads must first be converged before `__shfl_down_sync` works properly. And the mask should include the source thread. For subgroup size 16, we could use: `__shfl_down_sync(0x0000FFFF, val, offset)` for lanes 0-15 and `__shfl_down_sync(0xFFFF0000, val, offset)` for lanes 16-31. But we can't have different masks in different lanes within the same warp executing the same instruction. Actually, we can do it with `if/else` or `_sync` calls based on lane ID, but that's messy. Simpler: do the reduction in shared memory. ```cuda // After computing partial sums, write to a shared memory array __shared__ float partial_sums[48 * 16]; // or dynamically allocated // Each active thread writes its partial sum if (group_id < (K+G)) { partial_sums[group_id * group_size + lane] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Then do reduction in shared memory if (group_id < (K+G) && lane < group_size/2) { // parallel reduction within each group float v = partial_sums[group_id * group_size + lane]; for (int offset = group_size/2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { if (lane < offset) { v += partial_sums[group_id * group_size + lane + offset]; partial_sums[group_id * group_size + lane] = v; } __syncthreads(); // careful with __syncthreads inside conditional } } ``` Hmm, `__syncthreads` inside `if` is dangerous. We can restructure: ```cuda // write all partial sums if (group_id < (K+G)) { partial_sums[group_id * group_size + lane] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Tree reduction: for offset = 8, 4, 2, 1 for (int offset = group_size / 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { if (group_id < (K+G) && lane < offset) { partial_sums[group_id * group_size + lane] += partial_sums[group_id * group_size + lane + offset]; } __syncthreads(); } if (group_id < (K+G) && lane == 0) { float final_val = partial_sums[group_id * group_size]; // apply BN assignment_smem[group_id] = final_val; } ``` This is clean and safe. `group_size = 16`, so `partial_sums` needs `48 * 16 = 768` floats = 3072 bytes. Very small. But wait, each iteration of the loop over n has a `__syncthreads()`. And we have N=100 iterations. So 100 * (several syncs) = ~300-400 syncs per block. That's a lot of overhead, but each sync is fast. Actually, the `partial_sums` SMEM can reuse space. We can allocate it in the SMEM layout: - cluster_smem: D*(K+G) = 24576 floats - vlad_smem: K*D = 16384 floats - x_row_smem: D = 512 floats - assignment_smem: K+G = 48 floats - a_sum_smem: K = 32 floats - partial_sums: 768 floats Total: ~42240 floats = 169KB. Still under 227KB. Wait, 24576 + 16384 + 512 + 48 + 32 + 768 = 42320 floats. 42320 * 4 = 169,280 bytes. Under 227KB. Good. But we're processing N=100 iterations. Each iteration: 1. Load x row: all threads participate 2. Compute dot products: 768 threads active 3. Reduce: active threads participate 4. BN+softmax: 1 thread (or 48 threads for BN after reduction, then 1 thread for softmax) 5. a_sum atomic: 32 threads 6. vlad accumulation: all 1024 threads participate With all these syncs, the block is kept busy. Since only one block runs per SM (shared memory limited), and each SM has many warps (32 in our block), the warp scheduler can switch between warps during syncs. Actually, do we even need the BN running stats? Let me just code it up for eval mode first. But wait - I just realized something important. On H100, FP32 Tensor Cores support TF32 which is much faster. But this kernel is doing manual dot products in FP32 without Tensor Cores. While cuBLAS would use Tensor Cores. For a 512-element dot product, Tensor Cores don't help much because the problem is too small. But for the big matmul (204800, 512) @ (512, 48), cuBLAS uses warp-specialized kernels that are very efficient. So the question remains: will our manual GEMV approach be faster than using cuBLAS for the big matmul + custom elementwise kernel? Let's estimate: - cuBLAS big matmul: ~0.02ms (TF32 Tensor Cores, highly optimized) - Memory round-trip for assignment: ~0.02ms read + 0.02ms write - Custom BN/softmax/slice kernel: ~0.05ms - cuBLAS bmm: ~0.02ms - Memory round-trip: ~0.05ms - Custom subtract/normalize kernel: ~0.05ms Total: ~0.18ms - Fused kernel: - HBM read x: 0.12ms (bandwidth bound) - Compute: manual GEMV 5B FMAs. At 50% utilization of 67 TFLOPS: 0.15ms. - Total: ~0.25ms Hmm, the fused kernel might actually be SLOWER than using cuBLAS because our manual GEMV is inefficient! To make the fused kernel faster, we need to either: 1. Make the GEMV really efficient 2. Or avoid computing it manually Wait, but can we process multiple n values simultaneously to amortize the GEMV overhead? Actually, that's an excellent idea! Instead of processing one n at a time, process a group of n values. For example, process 4 n values at a time. Load 4 x rows, compute 4 assignment rows, then update vlad accumulators for all 4. But that increases SMEM usage for x_row and assignment. Still manageable: - 4 x rows: 4 * 512 * 4 = 8KB - 4 assignments: 4 * 48 * 4 = 768 bytes Total still small. But the real question is whether interleaving the computation helps. Within a block, we have 1024 threads. If we process 4 n's: - We could assign 256 threads per n for the dot products. - 256 threads per n * 4 n's = 1024 threads. - For each n, 256 threads compute 48 c values. 256/48 = 5.33 threads per c. Not ideal. Or we can have one warp (32 threads) handle dot products for one n. 4 n's = 4 warps = 128 threads. Underutilized. Or, think about it: a warp can compute one c value using 32 threads for the 512-element dot product. One warp per c. For 48 c's: 48 warps. But 48 warps = 1536 threads > 1024. Not possible in one block. What if we use multiple blocks per batch element? Say 2 blocks per batch element, each handling 50 n's. - Block 0: n=0..49 - Block 1: n=50..99 But then we need to merge the vlad accumulators and a_sum. This requires atomics or another reduction kernel. Actually, we can use 2 blocks per batch, and have them accumulate into the same output using atomics... vlad is K*D = 16384 floats. 2 blocks means 16384 atomic adds per n per block. With 50 n's, that's 819K atomics per block. Too many. Or we can do a two-level approach: each block produces a partial vlad, then reduce. But now we're introducing more complexity and HBM traffic. Another idea: process the dot products using a completely different strategy. The matrix x[b] is (100, 512) and clusters is (512, 48). The product is (100, 48). With 1024 threads, we can think of this as: - 1024 threads / 48 c's = 21 threads per c - Or tile the (100, 48) output into tiles. - For example, tile size = 4 x 4 = 16 elements. - 100*48 / 16 = 300 tiles. - With 1024 threads, each thread handles ~0.3 tiles. That's not great. - Or each block handles a few tiles per n. Actually, with B=2048 and N=100, the total number of output elements in assignment is 204800 * 48 = 9.8M. Each output element is a dot product of 512 elements. If we had one thread per output element, each thread would do 512 FMAs. Total threads needed: 9.8M. We can launch 9.8M threads! CUDA handles millions of threads easily. 9.8M / 1024 = ~9600 blocks. Or we can have each thread compute multiple elements. Wait, but that's for the raw matmul without BN and softmax. The problem is BN and softmax require reductions over the 48 elements of each row. So we can't process output elements completely independently unless we do the reduction later. Actually, what if we launch a kernel with 204800 blocks (one per row), each with 256 threads: - 256 threads compute 48 dot products for the row using parallel reduction. - 48 groups of ~5 threads each. Each thread computes ~512/5 = 102 FMAs. - Reduce within group (5 threads - warp shuffle or shared mem). - Then 1 thread does BN on the 48 values. - Then 1 thread does softmax on the 48 values. - Then all 256 threads write 32 output values and accumulate vlad... But vlad accumulation is over all n for a given batch. If each block handles one (b, n) pair, then vlad is a global accumulator that needs atomics across blocks. Hmm, a_sum similarly. So one block per (b, n) doesn't work well for vlad because vlad is per-batch. What if we do one block per batch element (b), and within the block, we process the 100 rows with all 1024 threads in parallel for the dot products? Wait, x[b, n, :] is different for each n. So we can't compute dot products for all n simultaneously in a straightforward way unless we load all x[b, :, :] which is 200KB and doesn't fit. Actually, we can process the rows in chunks. x[b, :, :] = 100 x 512. What if we only load a chunk of rows at a time into shared memory? For example, 16 rows at a time: 16 * 512 = 8192 floats = 32KB. Plus clusters 98KB. Plus vlad 65KB. Total: 195KB. Fits! Then for 16 rows: - Load 16 rows of x into SMEM (32KB). - Compute 16 assignment rows (16 x 48 = 768 values). - With 1024 threads, each thread handles ~0.75 assignment values. Not ideal. - Or each thread computes one or more full dot products. 1024 threads can handle 1024 of the 768 values, so some threads are idle. Wait, 16 rows * 48 c's = 768 dot products. Each dot product is 512 FMAs. With 1024 threads, we could assign one thread per dot product. 768 threads active, 256 idle. Each active thread does 512 FMAs. Then need to do BN and softmax per row. Then all 1024 threads do vlad accumulation. But processing 16 rows at a time means we need to store 16 assignment rows (768 floats = 3KB) before doing vlad accumulation. That's fine. Let me outline this: ```cuda __shared__ float x_chunk[CHUNK_SIZE * D]; // e.g., 16 * 512 = 8192 floats = 32KB __shared__ float assign_chunk[CHUNK_SIZE * (K+G)]; // 16 * 48 = 768 floats = 3KB for (int n_base = 0; n_base < N; n_base += CHUNK_SIZE) { int n_end = min(n_base + CHUNK_SIZE, N); int n_count = n_end - n_base; // Load x chunk for (int i = tid; i < n_count * D; i += block_size) { int n_offset = i / D; int d = i % D; int global_n = n_base + n_offset; x_chunk[i] = x[b * N * D + global_n * D + d]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute assignment for each row in chunk for (int idx = tid; idx < n_count * (K+G); idx += block_size) { int n_offset = idx / (K+G); int c = idx % (K+G); int global_n = n_base + n_offset; float val = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { val += x_chunk[n_offset * D + d] * clusters_smem[d * (K+G) + c]; } // BN val = (val - bn_rm[c]) * rsqrtf(bn_rv[c] + bn_eps) * bn_weight[c] + bn_bias[c]; assign_chunk[idx] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Softmax per row // Each row has 48 values. We can have one thread per row, or a warp per row. for (int idx = tid; idx < n_count; idx += block_size) { // find max float max_val = assign_chunk[idx * (K+G)]; for (int c = 1; c < (K+G); c++) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, assign_chunk[idx * (K+G) + c]); } float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < (K+G); c++) { float e = expf(assign_chunk[idx * (K+G) + c] - max_val); assign_chunk[idx * (K+G) + c] = e; sum_exp += e; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int c = 0; c < (K+G); c++) { assign_chunk[idx * (K+G) + c] *= inv_sum; } } __syncthreads(); // Accumulate a_sum and vlad // For a_sum: sum over n_count rows for (int c = tid; c < K; c += block_size) { float s = 0.0f; for (int n_offset = 0; n_offset < n_count; n_offset++) { s += assign_chunk[n_offset * (K+G) + c]; } a_sum_smem[c] += s; // no atomic needed, each c handled by one thread } // vlad accumulation // For each n_offset in chunk, for each c, d: // vlad[c, d] += assign_chunk[n_offset, c] * x_chunk[n_offset, d] for (int idx = tid; idx < K * D; idx += block_size) { int c = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float acc = 0.0f; for (int n_offset = 0; n_offset < n_count; n_offset++) { acc += assign_chunk[n_offset * (K+G) + c] * x_chunk[n_offset * D + d]; } vlad_smem[c * D + d] += acc; // no atomic needed, each thread handles unique idx } __syncthreads(); } ``` Wait, `a_sum_smem[c] += s;` - what if multiple threads try to write the same c? No, because the thread assignment is `c = tid; c < K; c += block_size`, so each c is only handled by one thread (since K=32 and block_size=1024, only 32 threads will execute this, each with a unique c). Similarly for vlad: `idx = tid; idx < K*D; idx += block_size` gives each thread a unique set of idx values. No conflicts. This is a huge insight! We don't need atomics at all for the vlad or a_sum accumulation if we ensure each thread handles the same (c, d) across all n values. And for the assignment computation, we have `n_count * (K+G)` threads worth of work. With CHUNK_SIZE=16, that's 768 items. With block_size=1024, 768 threads are active, 256 idle. That's 75% utilization. This chunk-based approach is much better! And we avoid atomics completely. Compute vlad into vlad_smem (accumulation across n) 2. Subtract clusters2 (elementwise) 3. Normalize 1 (per d over c) 4. Normalize 2 (per b over all) 5. Write to output We need vlad_smem as a scratchpad for steps 2-5. That's fine. Let me finalize the SMEM layout and write the kernel. SMEM Layout: ``` [0] clusters_smem: D * (K+G) floats [clusters_size] x_chunk: CHUNK_SIZE * D floats [x_chunk_size] assign_chunk: CHUNK_SIZE * (K+G) floats [assign_chunk_size] vlad_smem: K * D floats [vlad_size] a_sum_smem: K floats ``` Wait, clusters_smem + clusters2_smem? I decided not to preload clusters2. Total SMEM usage: clusters: 24576 x_chunk: 20*512 = 10240 assign_chunk: 20*48 = 960 vlad: 16384 a_sum: 32 Total floats: 52192 * 4 = 208,768 bytes. On H100, shared memory has 32 banks. The access patterns need to be bank-conflict-free. For x_chunk[n*D + d], if threads access different d's, they access different banks as long as the stride is odd or we use float4. With float (4 bytes), bank = address / 4 % 32. If consecutive threads access consecutive d's, they access consecutive banks. Good. For assign_chunk[n*C + c], similar. For vlad_smem[c*D + d], consecutive d means stride D=512. 512*4 = 2048 bytes. 2048 / 4 = 512. 512 % 32 = 0. So all threads accessing the same c but consecutive d would hit the same bank! That's a bank conflict! In the vlad accumulation: `assign_chunk[n*C + c] * x_chunk[n*D + d]` is added to `vlad_smem[c*D + d]`. Thread tid handles idx = tid + m*block_size. For a fixed m, idx values might have the same c but different d, or different c. c = idx / D, d = idx % D. Since D=512, consecutive idx values have the same c for 512 values. With 1024 threads, thread 0 and thread 1 might have: - idx 0: c=0, d=0 - idx 1: c=0, d=1 ... - idx 511: c=0, d=511 - idx 512: c=1, d=0 So threads 0..511 all access vlad_smem with c=0, d=0..511. The write address is c*D + d = 0*512 + d = d. Since d ranges from 0 to 511, consecutive d means consecutive addresses. BUT bank = (base + d*4) / 4 % 32 = (base/4 + d) % 32. For threads 0..511 accessing d=0..511: thread tid accesses bank (base/4 + tid) % 32. This is consecutive banks! No conflicts! Wait, I said vlad_smem[c*D + d] has stride D=512 between consecutive c. But WITHIN a fixed c, consecutive d are consecutive in memory (stride 1). So no bank conflict when threads access adjacent d for the same c. What about different c? Thread 511: c=0, d=511. Thread 512: c=1, d=0. Address 511 and address 512. Banks: 511%32 = 15, 512%32 = 0. Different banks. Good. Actually, vlad_smem is just a flat array. Thread tid accesses `vlad_smem[idx]`. For consecutive threads, idx differs by 1. So consecutive threads access consecutive memory locations. No bank conflicts! In the normalization step: ```cuda for (int d = tid; d < D; d += block_size) { for (int c = 0; c < K; c++) { float v = vlad_smem[c * D + d]; } } ``` Thread tid accesses vlad_smem[c*D + tid] for c=0..K-1. For consecutive threads, addresses are (c*D + tid). The difference between thread tid and tid+1 is 1. So consecutive threads access consecutive addresses. No bank conflicts! Great. However, when we write to output, the layout is transposed: ```cuda out_ptr[d * K + c] = vlad_smem[c * D + d]; ``` Consecutive threads (with same c, consecutive d) will write to: - thread 0: d*K + c - thread 1: (d+1)*K + c Stride is K = 32. 32*4 = 128 bytes. 128/4 = 32. 32%32 = 0. So threads 0 and 1 access addresses differing by 128 bytes. Since bank size is 4 bytes and there are 32 banks, 128 bytes = 32 banks. So threads 0 and 1 access the SAME bank! This is a bank conflict for writes. In fact, K=32 means the write stride is exactly 32 floats = 128 bytes = 32 bank widths. So ALL threads writing with stride K will conflict! To fix this, we can use a different thread mapping for the output write, or we can write in a coalesced pattern and then... no, the output layout is fixed. Wait, the output order is (B, D*K) where for each d, the K values are contiguous. In PyTorch, this is row-major: output[b, d*K + k] = vlad[b, d, k]. And vlad in SMEM is c*D + d (which is k*D + d if we rename c to k). So output[b, d*K + k] = vlad_smem[k*D + d]. To write coalesced: - Consecutive threads should write to consecutive output locations. - Consecutive output locations have the same d and consecutive k. - So consecutive threads should have the same d and consecutive k. But in our loop `for (int idx = tid; idx < K*D; idx += block_size)`, consecutive idx increments c by 1 and keeps d the same (for 512 values), then increments d. If we restructure so that we iterate in output-major order: For each b, the output is D*K elements. Thread tid handles elements where the output index = tid, tid+1024, etc. Output index = d*K + k. So for tid=0: d=0, k=0 tid=1: d=0, k=1 ... tid=31: d=0, k=31 tid=32: d=1, k=0 If we iterate in this order, then for the vlad read: vlad_smem[k*D + d]. For consecutive threads (consecutive tid = consecutive output index): - tid=0: k=0, d=0 -> vlad[0] - tid=1: k=1, d=0 -> vlad[D] - tid=2: k=2, d=0 -> vlad[2D] ... These are stride D=512! Bank conflict for reads. Hmm, there's a fundamental conflict because the internal layout (K, D) and output layout (D, K) are transposes. We can resolve this by either: 1. Transposing vlad in shared memory before writing 2. Using vectorized operations (float4) to mitigate 3. Just accepting the conflict since total data is small Actually, the total write is 128MB for all batches. At 3.35TB/s, even with some bank conflicts, it should be very fast. Or we can use shared memory to stage a transposed write. Alternative: in the subtraction step and normalization steps, keep vlad in (D, K) layout in SMEM instead of (K, D). Wait, but the vlad accumulation from assignment and x is natural in (K, D): vlad[k, d] += assign[n, k] * x[n, d] If we store as (D, K): vlad[d, k] += assign[n, k] * x[n, d] Then consecutive k's are consecutive in memory. And when we write output, consecutive k's for fixed d are also consecutive! This is perfect! Let's switch to (D, K) layout for vlad_smem! vlad_smem[d * K + c] Vlad accumulation: ```cuda for (int idx = tid; idx < K * D; idx += block_size) { int c = idx / D; // No, if layout is D x K, index = d*K + c // Hmm, our idx was c*D + d which maps to blocks of D. // For D*K layout, we need idx = d*K + c. } ``` Wait, if we iterate idx = 0..K*D-1 in any order, the accumulation is just adding to vlad_smem[idx]. The layout doesn't matter for accumulation. But for后续的 normalization: Norm 1: per d, normalize over K. If vlad_smem is (D, K): vlad_smem[d*K + c] ```cuda for (int d = tid; d < D; d += block_size) { float sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < K; c++) { float v = vlad_smem[d * K + c]; sq_sum += v * v; } float inv_norm = 1.0f / fmaxf(sqrtf(sq_sum), 1e-12f); for (int c = 0; c < K; c++) { vlad_smem[d * K + c] *= inv_norm; } } ``` Thread tid accesses vlad_smem[tid*K + c] for c=0..K-1. For c=0: address tid*K. With K=32, tid*32. Adjacent threads have addresses differing by 32 floats = 128 bytes = 32 bank widths. All threads hit the same bank! Bank conflict! If vlad_smem is (K, D): vlad_smem[c*D + d] Thread tid accesses vlad_smem[c*D + tid] for c=0..K-1. For c=0: address tid. Adjacent threads differ by 1. No bank conflict. For c=1: address D + tid. Adjacent threads differ by 1. No bank conflict. This is great for reads. But writes to output are strided by K. So (K, D) layout is good for the normalize read, but bad for output write. (D, K) layout is good for output write coalescing, but bad for normalize read. Let's think about how to fix the normalize read for (D, K) layout: We want threads to access consecutive elements for the same d. If thread tid handles d, then for varying c, the addresses are d*K + c. These are consecutive! But adjacent threads handle adjacent d: thread 0 handles d=0, thread 1 handles d=1. Thread 0 reads d=0: addresses 0, 1, 2, ..., 31 Thread 1 reads d=1: addresses 32, 33, ..., 63 These are all in different memory regions but adjacent threads don't access the same bank simultaneously because thread 0 accesses bank 0..31 (c=0..31 at base 0) while thread 1 accesses bank 0..31 (c=0..31 at base 32). Since thread 0 accesses all 32 banks over the loop, and thread 1 also accesses all 32 banks, there might not be conflicts across threads if each iteration accesses the same c. Wait, in the loop: For iteration c: - Thread 0 accesses address 0 + c - Thread 1 accesses address 32 + c Bank of address 0+c is c%32. Bank of address 32+c is (32+c)%32 = c%32. OH NO! Thread 0 and Thread 1 access the SAME bank in the same iteration! For the inner loop `for (int c = 0; c < K; c++)`, at iteration c: - Thread 0 (d=0): bank = c % 32 - Thread 1 (d=1): bank = (32 + c) % 32 = c % 32 So threads 0 and 1 conflict! In fact, ALL threads conflict on the same bank for each c! This is terrible. For (K, D) layout and normalization: Thread tid handles d = tid. At iteration c: address c*D + tid. Bank = (c*D + tid) / 4 % 32 = (c*512 + tid) / 4 % 32 = (c*128 + tid/4) % 32. For tid=0: (c*128) % 32 = 0 for all c (since 128 = 4*32). Wait, 128 mod 32 = 0. So for tid=0, address c*512. For tid=4: bank = c*128 + 1 = 1. Actually, bank = (address_in_bytes / 4) % 32 for 4-byte words. Address = c*D + tid floats = (c*512 + tid) * 4 bytes. Word index = c*512 + tid. Bank = (c*512 + tid) % 32. 512 mod 32 = 0. So bank = tid % 32. For consecutive threads, consecutive tid means consecutive banks! No conflict! And for different c, the bank is the same (tid%32), but we're accessing sequentially in c with the same tid, so same bank across iterations. That's fine, just serialized within a thread. One more thing: since we're using `load_inline`, I need to make sure the C++ code doesn't have any issues with Pybind11 or naming. The function name will be exposed via `functions=[...]`. Let me draft the Python module: ```python import math import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F import torch as th from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> #define CHUNK_SIZE 20 __global__ void netvlad_forward_eval( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ clusters, const float* __restrict__ bn_weight, const float* __restrict__ bn_bias, const float* __restrict__ bn_rm, const float* __restrict__ bn_rv, float bn_eps, const float* __restrict__ clusters2, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int N, int D, int K, int G ) { int b = blockIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int block_size = blockDim.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* clusters_smem = smem; float* vlad_smem = clusters_smem + D * (K + G); float* x_chunk = vlad_smem + K * D; float* assign_chunk = x_chunk + CHUNK_SIZE * D; float* a_sum_smem = assign_chunk + CHUNK_SIZE * (K + G); // Load clusters into shared memory int total_cg = D * (K + G); for (int i = tid; i < total_cg; i += block_size) { clusters_smem[i] = clusters[i]; } // Initialize vlad and a_sum for (int i = tid; i < K * D; i += block_size) { vlad_smem[i] = 0.0f; } if (tid < K) { a_sum_smem[tid] = 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); int num_chunks = (N + CHUNK_SIZE - 1) / CHUNK_SIZE; for (int chunk = 0; chunk < num_chunks; chunk++) { int n_start = chunk * CHUNK_SIZE; int n_end = min(n_start + CHUNK_SIZE, N); int n_count = n_end - n_start; // Load x chunk for (int i = tid; i < n_count * D; i += block_size) { int n_offset = i / D; int d = i % D; int global_n = n_start + n_offset; x_chunk[n_offset * D + d] = x[b * N * D + global_n * D + d]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute assignment for each (n, c) for (int idx = tid; idx < n_count * (K + G); idx += block_size) { int n_offset = idx / (K + G); int c = idx % (K + G); float val = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; c < (K + G); c++) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, assign_chunk[offset + c]); } float sum_exp = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < (K + G); c++) { float e = expf(assign_chunk[offset + c] - max_val); assign_chunk[offset + c] = e; Actually, the compiler might not unroll 512. But we can add `#pragma unroll 4` or similar to hint partial unrolling. Or we can manually unroll in chunks of 4 or 8. But single thread doing 512 iterations is fine, it's just a reduction loop. Hmm, can we optimize this further? Since x_chunk is in shared memory and clusters_smem is in shared memory, this is a SMEM-to-SMEM dot product. The bottleneck is that we have many threads doing independent dot products, which is good for instruction-level parallelism. But each thread has a long dependency chain of 512 FMAs. To break the dependency chain, we can accumulate in multiple partial sums: ```cuda float val0 = 0.0f, val1 = 0.0f, val2 = 0.0f, val3 = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; d += 4) { val0 += x_chunk[...] * clusters_smem[...]; val1 += x_chunk[...] * clusters_smem[...]; val2 += x_chunk[...] * clusters_smem[...]; val3 += x_chunk[...] * clusters_smem[...]; } float val = val0 + val1 + val2 + val3; ``` This gives ILP. But D=512 is divisible by 4. Let me add this. Actually, manual partial unrolling of 4 or 8 might help, but it also increases register pressure. In row-major, the memory of vlad is: for b in range(B): for d in range(D): for k in range(K): memory[b, d, k] Reshape to (B, D*K) means: for b in range(B): for i in range(D*K): memory[b, i] where i = d*K + k. Let me write this "post-softmax" kernel too. ```cpp __global__ void netvlad_post_softmax( const float* __restrict__ assignment, // B x K x N const float* __restrict__ x, // B x N x D const float* __restrict__ clusters2, // 1 x D x K float* __restrict__ output, int B, int N, int D, int K ) { int b = blockIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int block_size = blockDim.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* vlad_smem = smem; // K x D float* a_sum_smem = vlad_smem + K * D; // K // Initialize for (int i = tid; i < K * D; i += block_size) vlad_smem[i] = 0.0f; if (tid < K) a_sum_smem[tid] = 0.0f; __syncthreads(); // Accumulate over n for (int n = 0; n < N; n++) { // a_sum if (tid < K) { atomicAdd(&a_sum_smem[tid], assignment[b * K * N + tid * N + n]); } // vlad for (int idx = tid; idx < K * D; idx += block_size) { int c = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float a_val = assignment[b * K * N + c * N + n]; float x_val = x[b * N * D + n * D + d]; atomicAdd(&vlad_smem[idx], a_val * x_val); } __syncthreads(); } // Subtract, normalize, write // Same as before... } ``` Wait, in the post-softmax kernel, assignment is (B, K, N) and x is (B, N, D). For a fixed b and n: - assignment[b, c, n] is at index b*K*N + c*N + n - x[b, n, d] is at index b*N*D + n*D + d To avoid atomics, we can have each thread handle a fixed (c, d) and loop over n. ```cuda for (int idx = tid; idx < K * D; idx += block_size) { int c = idx / D; int d = idx % D; float acc = 0.0f; float a_sum_local = 0.0f; for (int n = 0; n < N; n++) { float a_val = assignment[b * K * N + c * N + n]; float x_val = x[b * N * D + n * D + d]; acc += a_val * x_val; a_sum_local += a_val; } vlad_smem[idx] = acc; if (d == 0) a_sum_smem[c] = a_sum_local; // only one thread per c should write a_sum } ``` Wait, multiple threads with the same c but different d will all compute a_sum_local. Only one should write it. Actually, a_sum only depends on c. We can compute it once per c by having a dedicated thread or by computing it redundantly and writing it with a condition. Better: compute a_sum in a separate loop to avoid redundant work, or just compute it redundantly. K*D=16384, N=100. Computing a_sum redundantly means each of the 16 threads per c computes the same a_sum. That's 16*100 = 1600 redundant additions per c. Total redundant work: 16*K*100 = 51200 adds. Negligible. So `BN(x @ W) @ W2` can be precomputed or at least the BN parameters can be fused. Wait, but the model parameters include the running_mean, running_var, weight, and bias of batch_norm. In eval mode: `BN(y) = (y - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * gamma + beta` So: `assignment = softmax(BN(x @ clusters))` We can't easily precompute this because x is the input. But we can create a fused operation by adjusting the clusters matrix and adding a bias: `BN(x @ W) = x @ W * gamma / std - mean * gamma / std + beta` `= x @ (W * gamma / std) + (beta - mean * gamma / std)` Actually, BN in PyTorch for 1D does: `y = (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * weight + bias` For matrix multiplication result, if we want to apply BN to each row element independently: If `z = x @ W` where z is (BN, K+G), then BN(z) operates on the last dimension. Let `inv_std = 1 / sqrt(running_var + eps)` Then `BN(z)_i,c = (z_i,c - running_mean_c) * weight_c * inv_std_c + bias_c` `= z_i,c * weight_c * inv_std_c + (bias_c - running_mean_c * weight_c * inv_std_c)` `= (x @ W)_i,c * scaled_weight_c + scaled_bias_c` Since `(x @ W)_i,c = sum_d x_i,d * W_d,c`, we have: `BN(z)_i,c = sum_d x_i,d * W_d,c * scaled_weight_c + scaled_bias_c` So we can precompute: `W_fused_d,c = W_d,c * scaled_weight_c` `b_fused_c = bias_c - running_mean_c * scaled_weight_c` Then `BN(x @ W) = x @ W_fused + b_fused` This is a standard optimization! Fusing BN into the preceding linear layer. This saves a lot of memory bandwidth and computation. Similarly, we might be able to optimize other parts of the computation. The condition for `.view()` in PyTorch is that there must exist strides for the new shape that are compatible with the original storage layout. More precisely: a tensor with shape (s0, s1, ...) and stride (st0, st1, ...) can be viewed as shape (t0, t1, ...) if and only if, for each element, the linear index computed using the old shape/stride equals the linear index computed using the new shape/stride. In C-order (row-major), element (i0, i1) in the 2D tensor is the same as element (i0*2 + i1) in the flattened 1D tensor. And element (j0, j1, j2) in the 3D tensor is element (j0*6 + j1*2 + j2) in the flattened 1D tensor. So we need: i0*2 + i1 = j0*6 + j1*2 + j2 for corresponding (j0, j1, j2) = unravel(i0*2 + i1, (2, 3, 2)). But the actual memory offset of element (i0, i1) is i0*3 + i1. In Linux, when a shared library is loaded, the dynamic linker looks for its dependencies using: 1. `LD_LIBRARY_PATH` 2. `DT_RPATH` / `DT_RUNPATH` of the loading binary 3. `/etc/ld.so.cache` search paths 4. `/lib` and `/usr/lib` Even if PyTorch (which is a .so itself) loaded a newer libstdc++ from `/opt/conda/lib`, when we load a NEW .so, the dynamic linker might still find the old libstdc++ in `/lib` first, UNLESS the new .so has an RPATH pointing to `/opt/conda/lib`. Since PyTorch's .so files probably have ORIGIN-relative RPATHs or were loaded with LD_LIBRARY_PATH set, they find the right libstdc++. But our freshly compiled .so might not have the right RPATH.
