So: vlad[d, k] = sum_n x[n, d] * assignment[k, n] In the original: vlad[k, d] = sum_n assignment[k, n] * x[n, d]. Then transpose: vlad[d, k] = sum_n assignment[k, n] * x[n, d]. These are the same! Because multiplication is commutative. So yes: `matmul(x.transpose(1, 2), assignment.transpose(1, 2))` gives (B, D, K) directly. This saves one transpose. Not a huge optimization, but it helps. So the optimized eval path is: ```python # assignment is (B, N, K) after softmax and slice a_sum = assignment.sum(dim=1, keepdim=True) # (B, 1, K) a = a_sum * self.clusters2 # (B, D, K) after broadcast # vlad computation vlad = torch.matmul(x.transpose(1, 2), assignment) # (B, D, N) @ (B, N, K) = (B, D, K) vlad = vlad - a # (B, D, K) ``` Wait, is `x.transpose(1, 2)` contiguous? x is (B, N, D), so x.transpose(1, 2) is (B, D, N) with strides (N*D, 1, D). For matmul, PyTorch/cuBLAS should handle transposed inputs fine, but let me check... Actually, for `torch.matmul` with 3D inputs, the batch dimensions must be broadcastable. If x is (B, N, D) and we want to matmul with something, using x.transpose(1,2) gives (B, D, N). The last two dimensions are (D, N). matmul of (B, D, N) with (B, N, K) gives (B, D, K). cuBLAS handles transposed matrices efficiently, but PyTorch might need to make them contiguous internally for the batch matmul. Let me check if there's a performance difference. Actually, looking at the reference again: ```python assignment = assignment.transpose(1, 2) # B x N x K -> B x K x N x = x.view(-1, max_sample, self.feature_size) # BN x D -> B x N x D vlad = th.matmul(assignment, x) # (B x K x N) x (B x N x D) -> B x K x D vlad = vlad.transpose(1, 2) # -> B x D x K ``` In the reference, `x.view(-1, max_sample, self.feature_size)` makes x into (B, N, D) explicitly. Then `assignment` is transposed to (B, K, N). Both are contiguous before matmul. If I use `x.transpose(1, 2)` which is (B, D, N), this is not contiguous (stride would be N*D, 1, D instead of N*D, D, 1). PyTorch's matmul might need to make it contiguous, causing a copy. So maybe the original approach is better. Let me stick with the original order for now and just do BN fusion. Actually, I can make it more efficient by keeping x in (B, N, D) shape throughout instead of the repeated view/reshape in the reference: - In the reference, x is first viewed to (B*N, D) for matmul - Then viewed back to (B, N, D) for the second matmul If I avoid these views by using `torch.matmul` on 3D tensors, I can save some overhead. But `torch.matmul` for 3D tensors calls into batched BLAS, which might have slightly different performance than 2D BLAS. Let me think... For the first matmul, the original does 2D matmul: (B*N, D) @ (D, K+G) = (B*N, K+G). This is equivalent to batched matmul: (B, N, D) @ (D, K+G) broadcasted. But PyTorch handles the 2D@2D case differently from the 3D@2D case. For 2D@2D, PyTorch calls `addmm` which uses cuBLAS GEMM with large matrices. For 3D@2D, PyTorch calls batched GEMM with B batches of (N, D) @ (D, K+G). With B=2048 and N=100, D=512, K+G=48: - 2D@2D: single GEMM of (204800, 512) @ (512, 48) -> very large M, small N/K. Good for cuBLAS. - Batched: 2048 GEMMs of (100, 512) @ (512, 48) -> small M, small N/K. Less efficient for cuBLAS because of overhead per batch. So the original's 2D@2D approach is actually MORE efficient for the first matmul! For the second matmul: (B, K, N) @ (B, N, D) = (B, K, D). This is batched GEMM with 2048 batches of (32, 100) @ (100, 512). M=32, N=512, K=100. This is reasonably efficient for batched GEMM. So the original's approach is already quite efficient for the matmuls. My main optimization is BN fusion for eval mode. Let me also consider: can I precompute `clusters_fused` as a buffer? ```python def __init__(self, ...): # ... self.register_buffer('_clusters_fused', None) self.register_buffer('_bias_fused', None) def _fuse_bn(self): if self._clusters_fused is None or self.training: # Recompute when needed with torch.no_grad(): std = torch.sqrt(self.batch_norm.running_var + self.batch_norm.eps) self._clusters_fused = self.clusters * (self.batch_norm.weight / std) self.bias_fused = self.batch_norm.bias - self.batch_norm.running_mean * self.batch_norm.weight / std ``` Wait, if I register as buffer with None, PyTorch might complain. The reference does: 1. matmul (big GEMM, compute-bound) 2. batch_norm (elementwise, memory-bound) 3. softmax (elementwise + reduction, memory-bound) Batch norm is elementwise and likely fused with the matmul output loading in the reference due to PyTorch's eager mode optimizations or because cuBLAS output is cached in registers/SMEM and then directly written out for the batch_norm kernel. Actually, in eager mode, matmul writes to memory, then batch_norm reads from memory. BN fusion saves one memory round-trip. For a tensor of size (204800, 48), that's about 39MB fp32. Reading 39MB and writing 39MB is about 78MB of memory traffic. At 3.35 TB/s, that's about 23 microseconds. On an H100, the matmul itself might take ~50-100 microseconds. Let me write the final code: ```python import math import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F import torch as th class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, cluster_size, feature_size, ghost_clusters): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.feature_size = feature_size self.cluster_size = cluster_size self.ghost_clusters = ghost_clusters init_sc = (1 / math.sqrt(feature_size)) clusters = cluster_size + ghost_clusters self.clusters = nn.Parameter(init_sc * th.randn(feature_size, clusters)) self.batch_norm = nn.BatchNorm1d(clusters) self.clusters2 = nn.Parameter(init_sc * th.randn(1, feature_size, cluster_size)) self.out_dim = self.cluster_size * feature_size def forward(self, x, mask=None): max_sample = x.size()[1] if x.device != self.clusters.device: msg = f"x.device {x.device} != cluster.device {self.clusters.device}" raise ValueError(msg) x_view = x.view(-1, self.feature_size) if self.training: assignment = th.matmul(x_view, self.clusters) assignment = self.batch_norm(assignment) else: # Fuse BN into linear layer for eval std = torch.sqrt(self.batch_norm.running_var + self.batch_norm.eps) scale = self.batch_norm.weight / std bias = self.batch_norm.bias - self.batch_norm.running_mean * scale clusters_fused = self.clusters * scale.unsqueeze(0) assignment = F.linear(x_view, clusters_fused.t(), bias) assignment = F.softmax(assignment, dim=1) assignment = assignment[:, :self.cluster_size] assignment = assignment.view(-1, max_sample, self.cluster_size) a_sum = th.sum(assignment, dim=1, keepdim=True) a = a_sum * self.clusters2 assignment = assignment.transpose(1, 2) x_view = x_view.view(-1, max_sample, self.feature_size) vlad = th.matmul(assignment, x_view) vlad = vlad.transpose(1, 2) vlad = vlad - a vlad = F.normalize(vlad) vlad = vlad.reshape(-1, self.cluster_size * self.feature_size) vlad = F.normalize(vlad) return vlad ``` Wait, for training mode, the weight `self.clusters` has shape (D, K+G), and `F.linear` expects weight of shape (out_features, in_features) = (K+G, D). So `self.clusters.t()` would be (K+G, D), which is correct for `F.linear`. My fused code uses `clusters_fused = self.clusters * scale.unsqueeze(0)`. `self.clusters` is (D, K+G), `scale.unsqueeze(0)` is (1, K+G), broadcasting gives (D, K+G). Then `clusters_fused.t()` is (K+G, D), which is what `F.linear` expects. Actually, for the original code `th.matmul(x_view, self.clusters)`, x_view is (BN, D) and clusters is (D, K+G), giving (BN, K+G). For the fused version: `clusters_fused = self.clusters * scale` where scale is (K+G,) broadcast to (D, K+G). Then `clusters_fused[d, c] = self.clusters[d, c] * scale[c]`. And `bias[c] = bias_c`. Then `F.linear(x_view, clusters_fused.t(), bias)` = `x_view @ clusters_fused + bias` = `sum_d x_view[bn, d] * clusters_fused[d, c] + bias[c]` = `sum_d x_view[bn, d] * self.clusters[d, c] * scale[c] + bias[c]` = `(sum_d x_view[bn, d] * self.clusters[d, c]) * scale[c] + bias[c]` = `matmul(x_view, self.clusters)[bn, c] * scale[c] + bias[c]` = `BN(matmul(x_view, self.clusters))[bn, c]`. Yes, this is correct for eval mode. One concern: in my fused code, I compute `std = torch.sqrt(...)` which is a tensor operation on a small tensor (48 elements). The overhead is negligible. After softmax on (204800, 48), the output is contiguous with shape (204800, 48) and stride (48, 1). Slicing to [:, :32] gives shape (204800, 32) and stride (48, 1). Can we view (204800, 32) with stride (48, 1) as (2048, 100, 32)? For any element [b, n, k], the memory offset should be b * 100 * 32 + n * 32 + k = b * 3200 + n * 32 + k. But the actual memory offset is i * 48 + k where i = b * 100 + n. So offset = (b * 100 + n) * 48 + k = b * 4800 + n * 48 + k. For these to match for all b, n, k: b * 3200 + n * 32 = b * 4800 + n * 48 => b * 1600 + n * 16 = 0 for all b, n This is only possible if 1600 = 0 and 16 = 0, which is false. So `.view()` should definitely fail. Wait... unless I have a bug in my reasoning about strides. Let me re-examine. A contiguous tensor of shape (R, C) has: - data pointer at some base address - element [i, j] has address base + i * C + j Slicing columns [:, :K] where K < C: - shape becomes (R, K) - stride remains (C, 1) - element [i, j] has address base + i * C + j Viewing to (B, N, K) where B * N = R: - element [b, n, k] should be at the same linear index as the flattened tensor - Flattened index of [b, n, k] in the viewed tensor: b * N * K + n * K + k - Memory address: base + (b * N * K + n * K + k) But the actual memory address from the slice is: base + i * C + k where i = b * N + n. Address = base + (b * N + n) * C + k = base + b * N * C + n * C + k. For these to match: b * N * K + n * K + k = b * N * C + n * C + k => N * K = N * C and K = C => K = C Since K < C, this is false. So `.view()` will fail. But the reference code DOES work. So there must be something wrong with my analysis. OH WAIT! I think I finally understand. In PyTorch, `.view()` might be more permissive in some cases because of how it handles storage offsets. Let me re-read the PyTorch documentation: "Returns a new tensor with the same data as the self tensor but of a different shape." "The returned tensor shares the same data and must have the same number of elements, but may have a different size. For a tensor to be viewed, the new view size must be compatible with its original size and stride, i.e., each new view dimension must either be a subspace of an original dimension, or only span across original dimensions d, d+1, …, d+k that satisfy the following contiguity-like condition that for all i = 0, …, k-1, stride[i] = stride[i+1] * size[i+1]." Ah! The key condition: "stride[i] = stride[i+1] * size[i+1]" For our sliced tensor: shape (R, K), stride (C, 1). Can we view as (B, N, K)? We need to split the first dimension (R) into (B, N). For dimensions [0, 1] with shape (R, K) and stride (C, 1): - stride[0] = C - stride[1] * size[1] = 1 * K = K - Is C = K? Only if G=0. So we can't split. Can we merge dimensions? Start with (B, N, K) and try to merge to (R, K). For (B, N, K) with strides (N*K, K, 1): - stride[0] = N*K, stride[1]*size[1] = K * N = N*K. So we CAN merge dims 0 and 1 into R=B*N with stride N*K. But our tensor has shape (R, K) and stride (C, 1), not (R, K) and stride (N*K, 1). Since C != N*K, it doesn't match. So `.view()` SHOULD fail. Unless... maybe the softmax output has a different stride than I think? Let me look at what PyTorch softmax actually returns. Actually, wait. I just realized: I might be wrong about F.softmax output strides. For certain implementations, softmax might return a tensor with the same strides as the input. But I already considered that. Hmm, actually, maybe in the reference code, `batch_norm` returns a contiguous tensor with a fresh allocation, and then `softmax` also returns a fresh contiguous tensor. But slicing and then viewing... it should still fail. Wait, let me look for an alternative: maybe `view` in PyTorch silently calls `contiguous()` when it can? NO, that's `.reshape()`. `.view()` definitely errors. Could the problem statement have a bug that was never caught because they use `.reshape()` in their actual test but wrote `.view()` in the reference? That seems unlikely. OR - and this just occurred to me - maybe `assignment` after `softmax` is NOT contiguous because `batch_norm` in eval mode does in-place operations? Actually, no, batch_norm in eval mode computes y = (x - mean) / std * weight + bias, which typically allocates a new tensor. Let me try another angle: what if the reference uses `th` (imported as `torch`) and there's some behavior difference? No, `th` is just an alias. OK, I'll just write my code to use `.reshape()` instead of `.view()` to be safe, and also add `.contiguous()` where needed. The numerical output will be the same. Wait, but the reference code is supposed to be the ground truth. If the reference uses `.view()` and it works in their PyTorch version, then using `.view()` in my code should also work. But if I use `.reshape()`, it will always work. The output is numerically identical. Let me write my code with `.reshape()` or `.view()` as appropriate, and add `.contiguous()` to be safe. Actually, for correctness, I want the exact same behavior. Let me just copy the reference code's operations exactly, only changing the first part for BN fusion. Here is my final code: ```python import math import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F import torch as th class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, cluster_size, feature_size, ghost_clusters): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.feature_size = feature_size self.cluster_size = cluster_size self.ghost_clusters = ghost_clusters init_sc = (1 / math.sqrt(feature_size)) clusters = cluster_size + ghost_clusters self.clusters = nn.Parameter(init_sc * th.randn(feature_size, clusters)) self.batch_norm = nn.BatchNorm1d(clusters) self.clusters2 = nn.Parameter(init_sc * th.randn(1, feature_size, cluster_size)) self.out_dim = self.cluster_size * feature_size def forward(self, x, mask=None): max_sample = x.size()[1] x_view = x.view(-1, self.feature_size) if x.device != self.clusters.device: msg = f"x.device {x.device} != cluster.device {self.clusters.device}" raise ValueError(msg) if self.training: assignment = th.matmul(x_view, self.clusters) assignment = self.batch_norm(assignment) else: # Fused BN + linear for eval mode std = torch.sqrt(self.batch_norm.running_var + self.batch_norm.eps) scale = self.batch_norm.weight / std bias = self.batch_norm.bias - self.batch_norm.running_mean * scale clusters_fused = self.clusters * scale.unsqueeze(0) assignment = th.matmul(x_view, clusters_fused) + bias assignment = F.softmax(assignment, dim=1) assignment = assignment[:, :self.cluster_size] assignment = assignment.view(-1, max_sample, self.cluster_size) a_sum = th.sum(assignment, dim=1, keepdim=True) a = a_sum * self.clusters2 assignment = assignment.transpose(1, 2) x_view = x_view.view(-1, max_sample, self.feature_size) vlad = th.matmul(assignment, x_view) vlad = vlad.transpose(1, 2) vlad = vlad - a vlad = F.normalize(vlad) vlad = vlad.reshape(-1, self.cluster_size * self.feature_size) vlad = F.normalize(vlad) return vlad ``` Wait, I changed `vlad = vlad.view(...)` to `vlad = vlad.reshape(...)`. This is safer. The reference used `.view()`, but `.reshape()` is equivalent when `.view()` would work, and falls back to `.contiguous().view()` when `.view()` would fail. For numerical equivalence, the result is identical. Actually, hold on. Let me re-check. I said `vlad = vlad.reshape(...)` instead of `vlad.view(...)`. Numerically, if `.view()` works, `.reshape()` gives the exact same result. If `.view()` would fail, `.reshape()` creates a copy, but the values are still the same. So yes, numerically equivalent. Let me compile this and test. Wait, I also used `assignment = assignment.view(...)` in the middle. I'll keep that the same as the reference for safety. Actually, looking at my earlier analysis, I was worried this might fail, but the reference code uses it and presumably works. So I'll use `.view()` exactly like the reference. For `vlad.reshape(...)`, the reference uses `.reshape()`, so I will too. One more thing: in my fused eval path, `th.matmul(x_view, clusters_fused) + bias` does `matmul` then `add`. The original does `batch_norm` after `matmul`. In fp32, the operations `((x @ W) - mean) * scale + bias` and `(x @ (W * scale)) + (bias - mean * scale)` are mathematically identical but might have slightly different rounding due to order of operations. Let me analyze the numerical difference: Original: for each output element y, y = (sum_d x_d * W_d - mean) * scale + bias Fused: y = sum_d x_d * (W_d * scale) + (bias - mean * scale) = (sum_d x_d * W_d) * scale + bias - mean * scale = (sum_d x_d * W_d - mean) * scale + bias Wait, `sum_d x_d * (W_d * scale) = scale * sum_d x_d * W_d` because scale is the same for all d in a given output channel. Scalar multiplication distributes over sum. So the fused version computes `scale * matmul_result + fused_bias`, where `fused_bias = bias - mean * scale`. Original computes `matmul_result`, then `matmul_result - mean`, then `* scale`, then `+ bias`. So the difference is: - Original: computes `matmul_result - mean` as an intermediate, then multiplies by scale. - Fused: computes `scale * matmul_result` directly. In exact arithmetic, `scale * (matmul_result - mean)` and `scale * matmul_result - scale * mean` are identical. But in floating point: `scale * (A - mean)` vs `scale * A - scale * mean` For fp32, `A` is the result of a large dot product (512 multiplications and additions). The subtraction of `mean` might lose some precision if `A` is large and `mean` is small. In the fused version, `scale * A` might also lose precision if `scale` is small. Actually, since `scale * (A - mean)` vs `scale * A - scale * mean` --- the difference between these is at most a few ULPs in fp32, which should be well within the tolerance of `1e-4` absolute/relative. Wait, but the matmul and the scaling aren't perfectly comparable because: - In the original: the matmul is done in cuBLAS, possibly with Tensor Cores or mixed precision internally. Then batch_norm does elementwise. - In the fused: the matmul might be done with scaled weights, which changes the operands but not the computation order. Actually, there's a more subtle point. In the original code: ```python assignment = th.matmul(x_view, self.clusters) # large GEMM assignment = self.batch_norm(assignment) # elementwise ``` The GEMM computes each element as a dot product of 512 numbers. If this is done using FMA (fused multiply-add) in fp32, the result is a single fp32 number. Then batch_norm subtracts mean and multiplies by scale. In my fused code: ```python clusters_fused = self.clusters * scale.unsqueeze(0) assignment = th.matmul(x_view, clusters_fused) + bias ``` The weights `clusters_fused[d, c]` are pre-scaled. The GEMM computes `sum_d x_d * (W_d * scale)`. Since `scale` is the same for each c, this is `scale * sum_d x_d * W_d` mathematically. But in floating point, `(x_d * W_d) * scale` vs `x_d * (W_d * scale)` vs `scale * (x_d * W_d)` can all differ slightly due to rounding. However, in a dot product of 512 terms, these differences average out and the final result should be within a few ULPs. Specifically, the difference between `matmul(x, W * scale)` and `matmul(x, W) * scale` in fp32 is typically very small because scale is a scalar applied to each output. cuBLAS GEMM computes `C = alpha * A * B + beta * C`. Here we could use `alpha=1` and add bias separately. But manually scaling W vs scaling the output could differ by about machine epsilon times the magnitude. Since fp32 has ~7 decimal digits of precision, and the absolute tolerance is 1e-4, differences of 1e-6 or smaller are acceptable. For typical value ranges (0 to 1 for inputs, small for weights), output values are likely within [-10, 10], so ULP differences of ~1e-6 should be fine. I'm fairly confident the fused version is numerically equivalent within tolerance. Let me compile and test this.
